## Transcript

- [00:02] Why do smart people make dumb decisions?
- [00:04] Why do conspiracy theorists think that we didnt land on the moon or that Hillary
- [00:08] Clinton is a space alien?
- [00:10] And why wont Bernice admit that the new Superman movie just isnt very good?
- [00:14] Weve talked about cognition before.
- [00:16] We usually refer to it as the process that we use to think and solve crossword puzzles
- [00:20] and stuff.
- [00:21] But really, cognition involves knowing, remembering, understanding, communicating, and to a certain
- [00:26] extent, learning.
- [00:27] And as truly wonderful as our brains are, we can be spectacularly bad at ALL of these
- [00:33] things.
- [00:34] We used to think our cognition worked like a computer -- logically processing information.
- [00:37] But that cabbage-sized chunk of pink, wet brain matter in your skull can do a lot more
- [00:42] than math, and the things that it does are certainly not always logical.
- [00:46] Many experts argue that its cognition that makes us truly human, and that everything
- [00:51] that comes with it -- our preferences, prejudices, fears, and intuitions -- are what make us
- [00:56] the individuals that we are.
- [00:57] Were not the only animals that show some evidence of cognition, of course: Chimps and
- [01:01] gorillas exhibit insight and planning; crows use tools; elephants teach each other.
- [01:06] But our capacity as humans to figure stuff out is matched only by our ability to totally
- [01:11] misjudge stuff.
- [01:12] As prone as we are to brilliance and insight, were equally likely to succumb to irrational
- [01:17] thinking and false intuition.
- [01:19] So, to borrow a riff from Rene Descartes, you think, therefore you are.
- [01:24] Which means youre brilliant a lot of the time.
- [01:27] And sometimes, youre just going to look stupid.
- [01:38] [INTRO]
- [01:40] We all want to make sense of the world.
- [01:41] And one of the major ways our cognition allows us do that is by forming concepts -- mental
- [01:46] groupings of similar objects, people, ideas, or events.
- [01:50] We like to lump things together.
- [01:52] Concepts simplify our thinking in such a fundamental way that we usually dont have to stop and
- [01:56] think about using them, theyre just there.
- [01:58] And yet without concepts, wed need a unique name for everything.
- [02:01] You couldnt just ask me to shake the anglerfish -- because thered be no concept of shake
- [02:06] or fish, let alone stuffed, blue anglerfish.
- [02:10] And if I told you I was devastated that I lost my anglerfish -- which I probably would
- [02:13] be -- Id also have to explain my emotions, their intensities, even the words themselves
- [02:19] that I had to use.
- [02:20] So basically, without concepts, no one would ever get anything done.
- [02:24] Wed all be like a bunch of ents taking all morning to say Hey, whats up?
- [02:29] We often organize our concepts by forming prototypes--mental images or pinnacle examples
- [02:34] of a certain thing.
- [02:35] For example, if I say bird--the general shape of a songbird probably pops into your
- [02:39] head before like, a penguin or chicken or emu, because robins and cardinals more closely
- [02:45] resemble our bird prototype.
- [02:46] Still, if I show you a picture of some crazy creature youve never seen before, and you
- [02:50] note that it has feathers and a beak, youll probably file it under the bird category because
- [02:54] it more closely resembles your concept of bird than your concept of rodent or overcoat
- [02:59] or footstool.
- [03:00] Concepts and prototypes speed up our thinking, but they also can box in our thinking, and
- [03:05] lead to prejudice if we see something that doesnt fit our prototypes.
- [03:08] A hundred years ago the sight of a female doctor might have caused some heads to explode,
- [03:13] because in peoples tiny minds, the prototypes of doctor and woman didnt have
- [03:17] any overlap.
- [03:18] And actually some people today still feel that way.
- [03:20] Haters gonna hate.
- [03:22] So its important to actively keep your mind open mind to make room for evolving concepts,
- [03:26] and remember that concepts may sometimes hurt as much as they help.
- [03:30] One of the biggest ways our cognition works to our benefit, though, is through our ability
- [03:33] to solve problems.
- [03:35] We use our problem-solving skills all the time: How to assemble Scandinavian furniture,
- [03:39] bake muffins with a missing ingredient, or handle the crushing disappointment of the
- [03:42] new Superman movie.
- [03:43] And we approach problem-solving in different ways -- sometimes we value speed; other times,
- [03:48] accuracy.
- [03:49] Some problems we figure out using trial and error--you know, you try something and if
- [03:52] it doesnt work, try it a different way, and keep at it until something works.
- [03:54] Trial and error is slow and deliberate--which may be good or bad, depending on the problem.
- [04:00] We can also use algorithms and heuristics to come up with solutions.
- [04:05] Algorithms are logical, methodical, step-by-step procedures that guarantee an eventual solution,
- [04:11] though they may be slow to work through.
- [04:13] Heuristics, on the other hand, are more like mental shortcuts -- simple strategies that
- [04:17] allow us to solve problems faster, although theyre more error-prone than algorithms.
- [04:21] Say youre at the store, looking for a family-sized bottle of Sriracha.
- [04:24] You could use an algorithm and methodically check every shelf and aisle in the store.
- [04:30] Or you could use heuristics and first search the Asian or condiment sections--the places
- [04:34] that make the most sense based on what you already know.
- [04:37] Heuristics may be way faster, but the algorithmic approach guarantees you wont overlook the
- [04:40] sauce along the way, because they stuck it in the deli or whatever dumb thing they did
- [04:43] this week.
- [04:44] So algorithms, heuristics, and trial-and-error are problem-solving strategies that involve
- [04:48] a plan of attack.
- [04:49] But sometimes we get lucky while puzzling out a problem, and Aha!, out of nowhere a
- [04:54] sudden flash of insight that solves our problem.
- [04:56] Ill use orange in the muffin recipe instead of lemon!
- [05:00] Or, Sriracha lives in the Mexican section!
- [05:02] For some reason!
- [05:03] Neuroscientists have actually watched that kind of sudden, happy brain flash on neuroimaging
- [05:07] screens.
- [05:08] In one experiment, they gave subjects a problem to solve:
- [05:11] What word can be added to the three words CRAB, PINE, and SAUCE to create a new compound
- [05:16] word?
- [05:17] Then they asked the subjects to press a button when they had the answer.
- [05:20] While the subjects thought about it, scans showed activity in their frontal lobes, the
- [05:24] areas involved in the focused attention of typical problem-solving.
- [05:27] But right at the Aha!
- [05:29] moment, just as they pushed the button, there was a clear burst of activity just above the
- [05:34] ear in the right temporal lobe, which, among many other things, is involved with recognition.
- [05:39] The answer, by the way, we already gave you the hint earlier in the episode.
- [05:44] Wheres my fish?
- [05:46] Those sudden bursts of insight are awesome, but you cant count on them to solve all
- [05:51] your problems.
- [05:52] And just because something feels, doesnt mean its truly correct.
- [05:56] Because as inventive and smartypants as we may be, our cognition often leads us astray
- [06:01] in all kinds of ways.
- [06:02] For instance, we often look for, and favor, evidence that verifies our ideas, while were
- [06:07] more likely to avoid or ignore contradictory evidence -- a tendency known as confirmation
- [06:12] bias.
- [06:13] This is really similar to the overconfidence weve talked about, when youre basically
- [06:17] more confident than you are correct.
- [06:18] When this kind of cognitive bias takes hold, you might cling to your initial conceptions
- [06:22] in a kind of belief perseverance, even in the face of clear proof to the contrary.
- [06:27] This happens all the time, and it can be maddening for people watching it happen.
- [06:32] People still think that the earth is flat!
- [06:35] Its like...WHAT?
- [06:36] HOW?
- [06:37] Theres space pictures!
- [06:38] I probably dont need to tell you -- people can really get weird and defensive when they
- [06:44] evade facts and choose to see only the information that confirms their beliefs.
- [06:48] They may even become functionally fixed, unable to view a problem from a new perspective.
- [06:53] Instead they just keep approaching a situation with the same mental set, especially if its
- [06:58] worked in the past.
- [06:59] Say youve got a nail sticking out from a board, and youre like I need to take
- [07:01] care of that!
- [07:03] Theres rocks, and bricks all around you.
- [07:06] But because of your functional fixedness on the idea that only hammers work on nails,
- [07:10] you dont even consider hitting it with the brick, and instead you waste a bunch of
- [07:13] time in the garage looking for a hammer, and youre angry and frustrated, and theres
- [07:17] still a nail sticking up from the board.
- [07:20] So, our mental set predisposes how we think, just as youll remember that our perceptual
- [07:24] set predisposes how we perceive.
- [07:27] This is what makes heuristics -- those super-convenient mental shortcuts that we all use -- so easily
- [07:32] fallible.
- [07:33] In the 1970s, cognitive psychologists Amos Tversky and Daniel Kahneman researched how
- [07:38] we make snap judgments, and discovered one way smart people make dumb decisions.
- [07:42] They found that people believe an event will be more likely to occur if they can conjure
- [07:46] up examples or memories of it, especially if those examples are particularly vivid,
- [07:51] scary, or awesome.
- [07:52] So, say youre in a casino and you win two dollars at a slot machine.
- [07:56] Suddenly every flashing light and ringing bell in the place goes off.
- [08:00] But when you lose -- which is the vast majority of the time -- its just...crickets.
- [08:04] With all their lights and noise-making, the casino makes sure that wins are super vivid
- [08:08] and memorable, while losses just go away unacknowledged.
- [08:12] That way, the next time youre standing there with 100 bucks in your pocket, youre
- [08:15] more likely to overestimate your chances of winning, because the memories of winning are
- [08:19] more striking.
- [08:20] The more mentally available those memories are, the more it seems that its going to
- [08:23] happen again.
- [08:24] This is known as the availability heuristic.
- [08:27] And it can warp our judgements of people, too.
- [08:29] If we keep remembering news footage that shows people of a given group shooting guns, that
- [08:34] can shape our impression of the entire group -- even if what we saw was only a tiny minority
- [08:40] within that group.
- [08:41] Essentially, we are great at fearing the wrong things.
- [08:44] We worry about being killed in a plane crash or getting bitten in half by a shark or accidentally
- [08:49] choking on a dumpling.
- [08:50] Thanks to our brains b-roll of horrific images, we come to fear whats actually
- [08:54] very rare, instead of worrying about much more common, but less memorable ends like
- [08:59] car accidents, cancer, and heart failure.
- [09:01] Our thinking can also be swayed by framing, or how an issue is presented.
- [09:05] Imagine youre considering climbing Everest or getting a nose job or eating a bowl of
- [09:10] raw blowfish.
- [09:11] I can frame the risks in different ways.
- [09:13] Telling you that youve got a 95 percent chance of survival sounds a lot different
- [09:16] than saying five out of a hundred people die doing this activity, though the information
- [09:20] is the same.
- [09:21] Our cognitive minds are capable of incredible intellectual feats and tremendous failures.
- [09:27] We can solve problems better than any organism on the planet, but given the chance, we can
